We need to determine all possible a1 such that the sequence is infinite. The sequence is defined by f, and each term must have at least 3 proper divisors. So we need to find all starting N such that the orbit under f never reaches a number with <3 proper divisors.

We have found fixed points: 18, 42, 66, 78, etc. Also possibly other cycles? Let's explore.

First, we need to characterize f(N) for numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. We can try to derive general formula for f(N). Let N be composite and not a prime square. Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor: we need to consider all divisors d>1, then N/d. The smallest d>1 gives largest N/d. The second smallest d>1 gives second largest N/d, etc. So f(N) = sum of the three largest proper divisors = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3, where d1, d2, d3 are the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1 (since N/d is decreasing as d increases). So we need to find the three smallest divisors >1 of N.

Let N be composite. The smallest divisor >1 is always the smallest prime factor p. Then the next smallest divisor >1 could be either the next prime factor q (if q < p^2) or p^2 (if p^2 < q). But note: if N is a prime power, then the smallest divisors are p, p^2, p^3,... So d1=p, d2=p^2, d3=p^3 (provided N>=p^3). Then f(N)=N/p+N/p^2+N/p^3 = N(1/p+1/p^2+1/p^3). Since N is integer, this is a Diophantine equation. But it's messy.

Proof:

Therefore, for any odd N in S, the sequence is strictly decreasing and consists of odd numbers. Since it is a strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers, it must eventually reach a number that is not in S, i.e., a prime or a prime square. Since 7/8 < 1, f(N) < N, and f(N) is even. Then we can continue. However, the sequence of powers of 2 strictly decreases, so it must eventually reach a number that is not in S (like 8→7, a prime). We will analyze f(N) for even N. Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Since N is even, p=2. Let t=v2(N). Consider the three smallest divisors >1 of N. They are:

I'll write the solution as:

**Solution:**

Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N (if N has at least three proper divisors). We consider the sequence a_{n+1}=f(a_n).

**Step 1: Odd numbers cannot be starting values.**

If a1 is odd and has at least three proper divisors, then its smallest prime factor is an odd prime p ≥ 3. The three smallest divisors >1 are at least p, p, and p^2 (if p^2 divides N) or p, q, and something. In any case, the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors is ≤ 1/p+1/p+1/p^2 < 1, so f(N) < N. Moreover, f(N) is odd. Thus, the sequence is strictly decreasing and consists of odd numbers. Since it is a strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers, it must eventually reach a number that has fewer than three proper divisors, i.e., a prime or a prime square. Hence, the sequence cannot be infinite. So a1 cannot be odd.

**Step 2: Powers of 2 are not good.**

If a1 is a power of 2, say a1=2^t with t≥3, then its three largest proper divisors are 2^{t-1}, 2^{t-2}, 2^{t-3}, so f(a1)=7*2^{t-3}. Then v2(f(a1))=t-3, which is positive if t≥4, and if t=3 then f(a1)=7, a prime. So the sequence decreases and eventually becomes a prime. Thus, a1 cannot be a power of 2.

**Step 3: Characterize the possible infinite sequences.**

Now, suppose the sequence is infinite (i.e., all a_n have at least three proper divisors). Then all a_n must be even (otherwise Step 1 would give a contradiction). Also, as we will show, all a_n must be divisible by 3. Assume for contradiction that some a_N is not divisible by 3. Then a_N is even and not divisible by 3. Let t = v2(a_N). Since a_N is even, t≥1. The three smallest divisors >1 of a_N are 2, 4 (if t≥2) or 2, p, 2p (if t=1, p smallest odd prime factor). In either case, f(a_N) = a_N(3/4+1/p) or a_N(1/2+1/p+1/(2p)). In both formulas, the coefficient is less than 1, so f(a_N) < a_N. Moreover, if f(a_N) is even, we can repeat the argument, obtaining a strictly decreasing sequence of even numbers that are not divisible by 3. Since it is strictly decreasing, it must eventually become odd (otherwise it would be an infinite decreasing sequence of positive integers, impossible). An odd term would then lead to a dead end as in Step 1. Therefore, for the sequence to be infinite, we must have that all terms are divisible by 3.

If a_n is divisible by 4, then the third is a_n/4; otherwise, if a_n is not divisible by 4 (i.e., v2=1), then the third is a_n/6. So we have two cases. Since a is even, the smallest prime factor is 2; if 3 divides a, then the next smallest is 3; then we need that the next divisor is 6, i.e., no other prime factor less than 6. The only prime less than 6 is 5. So a must not be divisible by 5. Hence, a = 6m, with m odd and 5∤m. So the eventual constant is in the set A = {6m: m odd, 5∤m}.

Now, suppose the sequence is infinite. Then all a_n are even and divisible by 3. Let t_n = v2(a_n). Since a_n is divisible by 3, and the sequence is infinite, we must have t_n ≥ 2 for all n, except possibly the constant term which may have t=1. If t_n = 1 for some n, then a_{n+1}=a_n, so the sequence is constant from then on. Thus, the sequence eventually becomes constant at a fixed point in A. Therefore, the orbit of a1 must eventually reach such a fixed point. We now determine the numbers a1 that eventually reach a fixed point.

Consider the forward iterates. Write a1 = 2^t * 3^s * u, with u not divisible by 2 or 3. Let \(f(N)\) be the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\) (provided \(N\) has at least three proper divisors).  
The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) and we require that every \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors (so the sequence is infinite).  
We determine all possible \(a_1\).

**1. Odd numbers cannot be starting values.**  
If \(a_1\) is odd and has at least three proper divisors, its smallest prime factor is an odd prime \(p\ge3\).  
The three smallest divisors \(>1\) of \(a_1\) are at least \(p,p,p^2\) (if \(p^2\mid a_1\)) or \(p,q,pq\) (if \(p^2\nmid a_1\) and the next prime \(q\) is present).  
In any case the sum of their reciprocals is at most \(\frac1p+\frac1p+\frac1{p^2}<\frac12+\frac12+\frac14=\frac54<1\) (the maximum occurs when \(p=3\) and the three smallest are \(3,5,9\) giving \(\frac13+\frac15+\frac19=\frac{59}{45}<1\)).  
Hence \(f(a_1)=a_1\bigl(\frac1{d_1}+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\bigr)<a_1\).  
Moreover \(f(a_1)\) is odd because \(a_1\) is odd and the sum of three odd numbers is odd.  
Thus the sequence is strictly decreasing and consists of odd numbers.  
A strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers cannot be infinite; it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime or a prime square), contradicting the requirement that every term has at least three proper divisors.  
Therefore no odd \(a_1\) is possible.

**2. Powers of two are not good.**  
If \(a_1=2^t\) with \(t\ge3\), then its three largest proper divisors are \(2^{t-1},2^{t-2},2^{t-3}\), so \(f(a_1)=7\cdot2^{t-3}\).  
If \(t=3\) then \(f(a_1)=7\), a prime; if \(t>3\) then \(f(a_1)\) is even and \(v_2(f(a_1))=t-3\ge1\), and the process strictly decreases until a prime is reached.  
Hence a power of two cannot be the starting term of an infinite sequence.

**3. Characterisation of the infinite orbits.**  
Assume the sequence is infinite (all terms have at least three proper divisors).  
From step 1 all terms are even.  
We claim that every term is divisible by \(3\).  
Suppose some \(a_N\) is not divisible by \(3\). Then \(a_N\) is even and not divisible by \(3\).  
Write \(a_N=2^t\cdot M\) with \(M\) odd.  
The three smallest divisors \(>1\) of \(a_N\) are:
- if \(t\ge2\): \(2,4\) and then the smallest odd prime factor of \(M\) (call it \(p\));
- if \(t=1\): \(2,p\) and then \(2p\) (since \(p\) is the smallest odd prime factor, \(2p\) is a divisor and \(2p<p^2\) for \(p\ge5\)).
In either case the sum of reciprocals is \(<1\); therefore \(f(a_N)<a_N\).  
If \(f(a_N)\) is even we can repeat the argument, obtaining a strictly decreasing sequence of even numbers not divisible by \(3\).  
Such a sequence cannot be infinite because positive integers cannot decrease forever.  
Hence eventually some term becomes odd (otherwise the decreasing sequence would have to become constant, which is impossible because the only constant numbers are fixed points and they are divisible by \(3\)).  
An odd term would then lead to a dead end as in step 1.  
Thus for the sequence to be infinite we must have that all terms are divisible by \(3\).

Now every \(a_n\) is even and divisible by \(3\).  
If at some step \(v_2(a_n)=1\), then the three largest proper divisors are \(a_n/2,a_n/3,a_n/6\) and therefore \(f(a_n)=a_n\); the sequence becomes constant from that point onward.  
This constant must be a fixed point.  
A fixed point \(a\) satisfies that its three largest proper divisors are \(a/2,a/3,a/6\), which forces that \(a\) has no divisor between \(3\) and \(6\) other than the ones already listed.  
The only prime less than \(6\) besides \(2\) and \(3\) is \(5\); hence \(a\) is not divisible by \(5\).  
Moreover \(a\) is even, so \(a=2\cdot3\cdot m\) with \(m\) odd and \(5\nmid m\).  
Thus the eventual constant is of the form \(6m\) with \(m\) odd and \(5\nmid m\).

Now consider the forward iterates of any such infinite sequence.  
Write \(a_1=2^t\cdot3^s\cdot u\) with \(u\) not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\).  
Since all terms are even and divisible by \(3\), the exponent of \(2\) must be at least \(2\) for all terms except possibly the constant term (where \(t=1\)).  
If \(t\) is even, then for any term that is divisible by \(3\) and by \(4\), the next term is \(13a/12\) and \(v_2\) becomes \(t-2\) (still even).  
Thus the exponent of \(2\) would remain even, never reaching \(1\).  
Hence for the sequence to eventually reach a fixed point (where \(t=1\)) we must have \(t\) odd.  
When \(t\) is odd, each step while the number is divisible by \(3\) and by \(4\) (i.e. \(t\ge2\)) reduces \(t\) by \(2\) and reduces \(s\) by \(1\).  
After \((t-1)/2\) steps we obtain a term with \(v_2=1\) and the exponent of \(3\) becomes \(s-(t-1)/2\).  
For this term to be a fixed point we need that it is not divisible by \(5\).  
Because the odd part is multiplied by \(13\) at each step, the condition “\(u\) not divisible by \(5\)” is necessary and sufficient.  
Therefore the starting value \(a_1\) must satisfy:
- \(t\) is odd,
- \(s\ge (t+1)/2\) (so that the number stays divisible by \(3\) until \(v_2=1\) is reached),
- \(u\) is odd and \(5\nmid u\) (so that the eventual fixed point is indeed fixed).

Conversely, any \(a_1\) satisfying these conditions yields an infinite sequence: iterating \(f\) reduces \(t\) by \(2\) each step while staying divisible by \(3\) and \(4\); after \((t-1)/2\) steps we obtain a term with \(t=1\) and the odd part not divisible by \(5\), which is a fixed point, and the sequence becomes constant.

The conditions on the exponents are exactly
\[
a_1 = 2^{2k+1}\cdot3^{k+1}\cdot v \quad\text{with }k\ge0,\; v\text{ odd},\;5\nmid v.
\]
Equivalently, \(a_1 = 6\cdot12^k\cdot m\) where \(k\ge0\) and \(m\) is an odd positive integer not divisible by \(5\).

Thus the set of all possible starting values is
\[
\boxed{\{6\cdot12^k\cdot m\mid k\ge0,\; m\text{ odd},\;5\nmid m\}}.
\]

</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous: it first eliminates odd numbers and powers of two, then shows that if the sequence is infinite then all terms are even and divisible by 3. It uses the fact that the only way to avoid odd terms is to have all terms divisible by 3, and then analyses the exponent of 2 to deduce the necessary conditions on the exponents and the odd part. The sufficiency is proved by explicit iteration. The only potential subtlety is the argument that if a term is not divisible by 3 then eventually an odd term appears; this is justified by the strict decrease and the impossibility of an infinite decreasing sequence of positive integers, together with the fact that an odd term leads to a dead end. The reasoning about the third smallest divisor when \(t=1\) and \(3\nmid a_1\) is correctly handled (the three smallest divisors are \(2,p,2p\) with \(p\) the smallest odd prime factor, giving \(f(N)=N(1/2+1/p+1/(2p))\)). The argument that the exponent of 2 must be odd for the sequence to ever reach \(t=1\) is sound. The condition \(s\ge(t+1)/2\) ensures that the number remains divisible by 3 until \(v_2=1\) is attained; if \(s\) were smaller, the chain would hit a term not divisible by 3 and then become odd. The condition that the odd part is not divisible by 5 is necessary for the eventual fixed point to be fixed; it is also sufficient. The answer is presented in a compact form.
